● Good — /tmp is created at build time with 1777. Now I have everything needed.

  Now let me design the reproducer carefully. Rather than a shell script, it
  should be a C program that:

  1. waitpid stress: fork N children that each exit immediately; reap each.
  Repeat K times. Tests sys_wait → sys_exit race.
  2. pipe stress: parent creates pipe, forks a child. Child writes M bytes in
  chunks, parent reads exactly M. Tests both sides of pipe wait queue.
  3. epoll stress: create a pipe, register read-end with epoll_wait(timeout),
  fork a child that writes after short sleep, parent wakes via epoll and reads.
  4. unix socket stress: bind/listen/accept/connect pair (socketpair would be
  simpler — let me check).

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

  autobind / abstract addresses, or if I just use a per-test unique path and
  unlink it.

  The simpler Linux-scale-correct approach: use AF_UNIX with a fixed tmp path,
  unlink before bind, unlink after. Let me also confirm pty exercise. Per plan
  9-6b, the pty reproducer needs an echo loop from bash-inside-makaterm, which

● Important findings:
  - task_wake_func clears twe->task = NULL for idempotency, but the 9-6 audit is
   about re-registration: the canonical pattern for signal-interrupted /
  condition-already-ready sleepers is to loop, which would re-init the
  task_we_t. That's fine.
  - wq_add does NOT reset we->next to NULL; the caller must init next before
  calling. The ALWAYS_INLINE helper task_we_init does set twe->we.next = NULL.
  And wq_add itself sets we->next = old_head inside the CAS loop. Good.
  - epoll_wake_func uses a plain *ewe->p_has_ready = 1; store. This is precisely
   the 9-6c target — needs atomic RELEASE. I'll fix it in task #4.

  Now sys_wait. Find it in syscall.c:

● Now I can see the exact sys_wait body. Let me analyze against the canonical
  pattern:

  for (;;) {
      // Phase 1 quick check: walk children, find zombie. If found, reap+return.
      // Phase 2 (register) + Phase 3 (re-check): MISSING
      sched_sleep();
  }

  The bug: sys_wait just calls bare sched_sleep() without registering on any
  wait queue and without re-checking after registration. It relies entirely on
  Phase 9-5's wake_pending scheduler flag to catch the race.

  Let me trace the wake path: what calls sched_wake(parent) on exit?

     // ── sys_exit ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────

● Now I see the full race chain for sys_wait. Key elements:

  1. task_child_add(g_init_task, child) is called from sys_exit during
  reparenting — this mutates another task's children list concurrently with 
  sys_wait. Let me check that function.
  2. signal_send(parent, SIGCHLD) is the wake trigger. Let's look at
  signal_send.

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

● Bash(sed -n '720,770p' kernel/proc/sched.c)
  ⎿  void task_child_add(task_t* parent, task_t* child) {
         child->child_next  = parent->children;
         parent->children   = child;
     … +26 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● This is the bug: task_child_add and task_child_remove are completely 
  unsynchronized. sys_exit reparents its children to init by calling
  task_child_add(g_init_task, child) — mutating init's children list —
  concurrently with init (or any other parent) walking its children list in
  sys_wait.

  Also sys_wait itself removes from g_current->children without any
  synchronization.

  Let me also check sys_fork / where task_child_add is called from the parent
  side:

● Bash(sed -n '585,610p' kernel/syscall/syscall.c; echo "---"; sed -n '940,955p'
       kernel/syscall/syscall.c)
  ⎿                                g_syscall_user_rip,
                                   g_syscall_user_rflags,
                                   g_syscall_user_rsp,
     … +21 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● So task_child_add(parent, child) is called by:
  - sys_fork with parent = g_current
  - sys_spawn with parent = g_current
  - sys_exit reparenting with parent = g_init_task (when the reparent target is
  init)
  - signal.c fatal-signal death path (line 185)

  And g_current->children = ... / g_current->child_next = ... unlinks happen in
  sys_wait.

  Now — is there already a lock for this? Check task_t:

  Searched for 2 patterns (ctrl+o to expand)

● Bash(sed -n '100,200p' kernel/proc/process.h)
  ⎿      int32_t       exit_code;    // set by sys_exit, readable via wait(
     )
     … +61 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● No children_lock. Children list is completely unsynchronized. Now let me think
   about the correct fix.

  The race

  Writers to parent->children:
  - fork/spawn: parent adds its OWN child (self-write, no remote CPU)
  - sys_exit: reparents its children TO g_init_task via task_child_add
  (cross-CPU write to init's list)
  - sys_wait: unlinks zombie from own children list (self-write)
  - signal.c fatal death: same as sys_exit (reparent to init + unlink from
  original parent's list)

  Readers of parent->children:
  - sys_wait: walks own children list to find zombies
  - sys_exit: walks own children list to reparent them (self-read)

  The cross-CPU races:

  1. Reparenting race: Child C on CPU X calls sys_exit. Its parent P is anyone.
  C's own children (grandchildren of P) are reparented to init. Init, on some
  CPU, might be walking its children list in sys_wait right then.
  2. Unlink-vs-exit race: Parent P on CPU A in sys_wait walks its children
  looking for zombies. A child C on CPU B just became a zombie (TASK_ZOMBIE
  write), then signal_send(P, SIGCHLD). P's walk may or may not see C's
  state=ZOMBIE; either is fine (retries on wake). But if parent walks and
  removes child C from its list, C is in its parent's list — no cross-CPU write
  to C's place in P's list needs protection from itself. But C's sys_exit
  touches C's own child_next only on its grandchildren, not on itself being in
  P's list.

  Wait, let me re-read sys_exit's actual children-list touches:
  - Line 494-506: walks g_current->children (self-read) and calls
  task_child_add(g_init_task, child) (cross-CPU write to init's list) OR sets
  child->child_next = NULL and children = NULL.

  So the cross-CPU conflict is only on g_init_task->children (init's list). And
  init, in sys_wait, walks its own children list.

  Actually — broader race: when a child exits and reparents its grandchildren to
   init, there's a concurrent walker of init's children. In real Linux this is
  protected by tasklist_lock or rcu_read_lock.

  Also: sys_wait unlink race: parent P in sys_wait finds a zombie Z in its
  children and removes it. Another child C of P on another CPU exits at the same
   time — C's sys_exit reparents C's children to init, but does NOT touch P's
  children list (P's list entries are P's OWN children, not grandchildren).
  Except: C itself is in P's children list. When C exits, C does NOT remove
  itself from P's children list; it just becomes a zombie and signal_sends P. P
  later reaps C via sys_wait's unlink.

  So concurrent writes to P's children list happen only when:
  - P's own fork/spawn adds children (P's CPU only — sys_wait and fork are
  mutually exclusive on same task)
  - P's sys_wait unlinks (P's CPU only)
  - Another task calls task_child_add(P, ...) — only happens when P is
  g_init_task, during reparenting.
  - Another task calls task_child_remove(P, ...) — let me check who calls this.

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

● task_child_remove is never called — dead code. Good. So the races on
  parent->children are:
  - Same-task writes (fork/spawn/wait): no cross-CPU race since g_current can't
  concurrently run on two CPUs.
  - Cross-CPU reparent-to-init: only affects g_init_task's children list. But:
  init runs on CPU 0 (always, via pick_home_cpu=0). Under 9-6 with round-robin,
  init could be anywhere. But more importantly, any task's sys_exit on any CPU
  may reparent its orphans to init, while init on CPU 0 walks its children in
  sys_wait.

  Plus there's a subtler race that applies to every parent, not just init: the
  children->child_next chain read in sys_wait versus a brand-new child being
  linked in by a concurrent fork by the same parent. But a task can't be in two
  places at once — fork is a syscall on the parent; sys_wait is a syscall on the
   parent; they can't concurrently execute.

  Unless — threads. Are threads implemented as sharing a task_t or separate
  task_t's with a shared tgid?

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

● Bash(sed -n '961,1065p' kernel/syscall/syscall.c)
  ⎿  // ── sys_thread ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
     ───────
     // thread(entry_ptr, stack_top, flags) → tid, or -1 on error.
     … +59 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● Crucial finding: sys_thread does NOT call task_child_add. A thread is a task
  with the same tgid/ppid/pgid/sid but is NOT added to any parent's children
  list. Good — so threads don't create a children-list race.

  But: threads with shared files/mm can both call fork/wait concurrently! Two
  threads in the same process call fork at the same time on different CPUs. Each
   thread is a separate task_t. Each calls task_child_add(g_current, child)
  where g_current is itself, not the shared "parent". So they update different
  task_t's children lists — no race.

  But wait: both threads have the same ppid. Does anything use children hanging
  off ppid? sys_wait uses g_current->children, which is the calling-thread's own
   list. So thread A's fork puts the new child in A's children list; thread B's
  wait walks B's children list. They can't see each other's children. This is a
  pre-existing semantic quirk (not POSIX-compliant, but consistent). Not my
  problem for 9-6.

  OK so my analysis:

  Remaining cross-CPU races on parent->children:
  1. Init's children list: any sys_exit on any CPU may reparent orphans to init
  via task_child_add(g_init_task, child). Meanwhile init could be in sys_wait
  walking its own children.
  2. (no other cross-CPU writer)

  Plus the sleep/wake race — sys_wait uses bare sched_sleep() with no wait queue
   registration. This is the canonical pattern violation the plan targets.

  The fix

  I'll apply the canonical pattern correctly. Since the plan says "zero new
  locks", I need to pick the right primitive.

  Option A: Add a wait_queue_t children_wq to task_t. Every sys_exit calls
  wait_queue_wake_all(&parent->children_wq) after signal_send(parent, SIGCHLD).
  sys_wait uses the canonical task_we_t pattern. Cost: one pointer field per
  task_t. One wait_queue_t per task is not a lock (the lock inside wait_queue_t
  is cold-path only for cancel). This matches pipe/pty. Plan says "zero new
  locks" — a cold-path remove_lock on a new wait_queue_t probably counts, per
  the LOCKS.md ledger convention.

  Option B: Reuse an existing primitive. sched_sleep + wake_pending already
  gives us the scheduler-level interlock. The canonical pattern's "register on
  queue" step's only purpose is to receive the cross-CPU wake. But signal_send
  already does sched_wake(parent). So in principle the bare sched_sleep is
  already woken by signal_send.

  Let me look carefully at this. The claim in phase 9-5 is: signal_send 
  unconditionally calls sched_wake → sets wake_pending on the parent's rq_lock →
   parent's next sched_sleep sees wake_pending and bails.

  But that only works if the parent hasn't already committed to sleep and read
  wake_pending=0. Let me check sched_sleep:

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

● Bash(sed -n '975,1070p' kernel/proc/sched.c)
  ⎿  void sched_sleep(void) {
         // Enforce: no task may sleep while preempt is disabled.  This is
     the
     … +62 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● OK, wake_pending already handles the "wake arrived just before sleep" case
  cleanly. So the remaining question is: in sys_wait's loop, is there any 
  interleaving of a child's exit that is NOT caught by this?

  Trace:
  1. sys_wait iteration N: walks P->children, sees no zombie.
  2. Child C on CPU X: sets state=TASK_ZOMBIE, sched_add_zombie, signal_send(P, 
  SIGCHLD).
  3. signal_send calls sched_wake(P) which takes P's home rq_lock. If P is not
  yet TASK_SLEEPING → sets wake_pending=1. If P is TASK_SLEEPING → dequeues from
   sleep_head, enqueues.
  4. sys_wait: reaches sched_sleep(). Takes rq_lock. If wake_pending: clears,
  returns, loops back to walk children.

  This is the scheduler-level protection. But does P's re-walk see C's
  state=ZOMBIE?

  The writes on CPU X are:
  - C->exit_code = ...
  - C->state = TASK_ZOMBIE (plain store)
  - sched_add_zombie(C) (takes lock)
  - signal_send(P, SIGCHLD) → sched_wake(P) (takes P's rq_lock, release on
  unlock)

  The reads on CPU P (after wake):
  - sched_sleep lock-release (acquire-pair with sched_wake's release)
  - walks g_current->children, reads child->state

  The release of P's rq_lock in sched_wake pairs with the acquire of P's rq_lock
   in sched_sleep. Every store made on CPU X before it took P's rq_lock is
  visible to CPU P after CPU P takes and releases the same lock. In particular
  C->state = TASK_ZOMBIE was written before signal_send → sched_wake on CPU X →
  globally ordered.

  Actually wait: C->state = TASK_ZOMBIE is a plain relaxed store.
  sched_add_zombie takes a lock (lock is acquire/release). Then signal_send →
  sched_wake takes P's rq_lock (spin_lock = acquire). The plain store
  state=ZOMBIE happens-before sched_add_zombie's lock ACQUIRE, which
  happens-before sched_add_zombie's lock RELEASE. Then signal_send's sched_wake
  takes P's rq_lock (different lock). Does the release on zombie_lock
  synchronize-with the acquire on rq_lock? No. Different locks don't order 
  across CPUs.

  However, on x86, all stores are TSO — plain stores to C->state are globally
  visible in program order with subsequent stores on the same CPU. And
  subsequent lock operations (lock xchg/cmpxchg) have full fence semantics. So:
  plain store C->state=ZOMBIE; then CPU X enters sched_wake(P) which does
  xchg/cmpxchg for the spinlock; that's a full barrier. Then sched_wake sets
  P->wake_pending=1 (plain store) and unlocks. Any subsequent acquire on P's
  rq_lock observes both.

  x86 is fine here due to TSO. On a weaker arch it would need explicit barriers.
   Since we're x86-only, the ordering is fine.

  So the sys_wait loop's re-walk WILL see the child's state=ZOMBIE, even without
   any wait queue. The canonical register→re-check→sleep pattern from the plan
  is there specifically to handle losing a wake in the window between "unlocked
  cond-check" and "sched_sleep". But Phase 9-5's wake_pending flag serves
  exactly that role — and it's cheaper because it doesn't require allocating a
  wait queue.

  The race the plan is really worried about for 9-6a is the children-list 
  integrity. Re-read entry 24:

  ▎ waitpid / zombie reap: the parent walks children (plain list, no lock) and 
  ▎ may observe a child in TASK_RUNNING that transitions to TASK_ZOMBIE one 
  ▎ instruction later, then the parent sleeps just before signal_send fires. 
  ▎ Relies on #23's wake_pending, but also relies on child->state being read 
  ▎ AFTER sys_exit's state = TASK_ZOMBIE store has propagated — memory barrier 
  ▎ placement needs to be spelled out.

  OK so entry 24 is primarily worried about the memory barrier, which — on x86
  TSO — is already handled for free by the lock acquire/release around both
  sides.

  The plan's 9-6a text is more specific:

  ▎ Parent's children list walk is unsynchronised. On UP it's fine (parent owns 
  ▎ the list), but on SMP the child mutates the list when it reparents-to-init 
  ▎ during its OWN exit. That write is ordered before the signal_send/wake, but 
  ▎ parent may be mid-walk when it happens.

  So the plan's concrete concern: init walking its own children list while any 
  exiting task on any CPU is concurrently reparenting to init. The reparent
  happens BEFORE the signal_send wake chain, so any parent that's currently in
  sched_sleep will be woken and re-walk with the new state. But a parent that is
   currently mid-walk (specifically init) can trip on a child_next pointer being
   updated under it.

  The plan's preferred fix (option b) says:

  ▎ The only concurrent mutator is the child's own sys_exit reparenting, and it 
  ▎ happens BEFORE the SIGCHLD / sched_wake. Memory barrier in signal_send's 
  ▎ lock release ensures parent's next walk sees the new links. Confirm with 
  ▎ reproducer.

  But this doesn't address mid-walk. If init is already mid-walk when the
  exiting child's task_child_add(g_init_task, child) runs, init can read a torn
  pointer. The plan's "option b" handwaves this by saying "the parent's next
  walk" — meaning, after a wake the walk is safe because all mutations
  happened-before the wake. But init might be in the middle of walking when the
  mutation happens without a wake yet — say, init is reaping zombie X, and while
   walking towards X, task Y on another CPU finishes exiting and reparents its
  orphans, mutating init's children head pointer.

  This IS a real race. The plan's "option b" is incomplete — or rather, relies
  on the fact that init mid-walk is already committed to finding X as a zombie,
  will find X (since X is reached via unchanged child_next pointers if X comes
  before the prepended new reparented child in the list), and will successfully
  reap X. The new reparented children are PREPENDED to the head (task_child_add
  uses children = child after linking child->child_next = children). So if init
  already read past the old head when task Y's reparent runs, init finishes its
  walk with the OLD chain; it never sees the new heads; the reparented children
  will be seen on the next walk.

  Is a prepend race actually safe? Let's trace:

  Before: init->children = A -> B -> C -> NULL.
  Init starts walk: reads children = A, then reads A->child_next = B, etc.
  Concurrent: task Y reparents children X1,X2 to init:
  - Write X1->child_next = B (old head... wait no, child->child_next = 
  parent->children)
  - Actually task_child_add: child->child_next = parent->children; 
  parent->children = child;
  - That's X1->child_next = A; init->children = X1; Then X2->child_next = X1; 
  init->children = X2;.

  Init, mid-walk, has already dereferenced init->children = A — it's walking
  from A. It never reads the new init->children = X2. So init sees A, B, C,
  NULL. Safe.

  But there's a reordering problem: if init does task_t* child = init->children;
   (reads A), then the writer does the two stores in order (release), init sees
  the old head. But without a barrier, init might re-read init->children on a
  subsequent iteration? No — sys_wait stores the head into a local child = 
  g_current->children at the start of the walk and then follows child = 
  child->child_next. Head is only read once.

  So the only remaining concern is: between two consecutive iterations of
  sys_wait's outer for(;;) loop (i.e., across a sched_sleep wake), can the list
  be corrupt? No — every writer holds some discipline: the prepend is complete
  (both child->child_next and parent->children = child are executed sequentially
   by the writer, and both become visible to any acquire on any lock the writer
  subsequently releases). Since signal_send/sched_wake takes P's rq_lock AFTER
  the reparent, and sys_wait acquires P's rq_lock in sched_sleep before walking
  on wake, the ordering is sound.

  Conclusion: The children-list walk under the current sys_wait is actually safe
   against concurrent prepends (task_child_add) by construction on x86. The
  plan's option (b) IS correct and complete for this specific use.

  But there's one subtle bug I still see: task_child_add does non-atomic pointer
   writes. On the reader side, init's child->child_next reads are also
  non-atomic. C11 says non-atomic concurrent read/write is UB. On x86
  plain-word-aligned pointer writes are atomic in practice (no torn values), and
   the compiler COULD optimize reads if it thought they were monotonic. But
  since we're walking through different tasks' linked list, the compiler can't
  reason across task_t boundaries. This is fine.

  However, more importantly: the plan asks us to spell out the memory barrier.
  Let me look for any smp_mb or release_store near children_list...

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

● task_child_add has no barrier. But on x86, the spinlock release in
  signal_send's rq_lock path provides full StoreStore+LoadStore ordering. Since
  the writer's sequence is:
  1. Plain stores: child->child_next = ...; parent->children = child; (TSO
  orders these in program order)
  2. Plain stores: child->state = TASK_ZOMBIE
  3. sched_add_zombie(child) — takes zombie list lock (full barrier on lock
  acquire; full barrier on release)
  4. signal_send(parent, SIGCHLD) — takes parent's rq_lock
  5. Sets wake_pending OR moves parent out of sleep list
  6. Unlocks parent's rq_lock (release)

  Readers on parent take parent's rq_lock in sched_sleep, release, then re-walk
  children. The lock-release→lock-acquire pair guarantees all writes made before
   the release are visible to the acquirer.

  So the writers' prior stores (including children list mutation) are ordered
  before the parent-side lock-release in sched_wake, and the parent-side
  lock-acquire in sched_sleep happens-after. But sys_wait walks children BEFORE 
  calling sched_sleep on the current iteration. So:

  - Iteration 1: walk finds nothing, call sched_sleep, which takes+releases
  rq_lock (acquire).
  - After sched_sleep returns, iteration 2's walk sees everything the waker
  wrote before its sched_wake(P).

  Iteration 1's walk might miss a just-added child, BUT iteration 2 won't miss
  it, because iteration 2 is after a release-acquire with any waker that called
  sched_wake(P). The only loophole: what if the waker's sched_wake sees P not
  sleeping (wake_pending set), and P's sched_sleep consumes wake_pending and
  returns — did sched_sleep take the lock?

  spin_lock_irqsave(&home->rq_lock, flags);
  if (self->wake_pending) {
      self->wake_pending = 0;
      spin_unlock_irqrestore(&home->rq_lock, flags);
      ...
      return;
  }

  Yes — it takes the lock. Lock acquire is a full barrier on x86. So iteration
  2's walk is memory-ordered to see the waker's writes.

  So the only thing missing from sys_wait vs the canonical pattern is explicit 
  documentation — the code already works because wake_pending + rq_lock provides
   the entire sleep-interlock and the x86 lock barriers provide the memory
  ordering. The register→re-check→sleep triangle would be redundant with
  wake_pending.

  This is a critical insight. Let me re-read the plan to confirm I'm reading it
  right... the plan for 9-6a says:

  ▎ Fix:
  ▎ 1. Keep the Phase 9-5 wake_pending protection (already in).
  ▎ 2. Make the children list modifiable only under the parent's per-task lock, 
  ▎ OR audit every mutator (fork, exit, reparent) to confirm it only runs when 
  ▎ the parent is either (a) the mutator, (b) quiesced in sys_wait just before 
  ▎ re-walking.
  ▎ 3. Preferred: option (b). The only concurrent mutator is the child's own 
  ▎ sys_exit reparenting, and it happens BEFORE the SIGCHLD / sched_wake. Memory
  ▎  barrier in signal_send's lock release ensures parent's next walk sees the 
  ▎ new links. Confirm with reproducer.
  ▎
  ▎ Deliverable: sys_wait uses the canonical pattern; no new lock.

  OK so option (b) is: audit the mutators, confirm by construction, document the
   barrier reasoning inline. The deliverable says "uses the canonical pattern"
  but the plan preferred option is "no canonical wait queue, just confirm via
  audit".

  I think the right action here is:
  1. Add a detailed comment block to sys_wait documenting exactly the ordering
  rules relied on, and the writer→reader chain. This is the "spell out the
  memory barrier" the plan demands.
  2. Verify my audit claim by reading every task_child_add caller under 9-6
  conditions.
  3. No code change to sys_wait's algorithm — it is already correct under option
   (b).

  Wait — but I should still check: is the walk actually safe when removing a
  zombie? sys_wait does:

  if (zombie_prev) zombie_prev->child_next = zombie->child_next;
  else             g_current->children     = zombie->child_next;

  This is a write to g_current->children (only the current CPU) OR a write to
  zombie_prev->child_next (a sibling task_t — also only from this CPU because
  only the current task can be mid-wait, and task_child_add only prepends new
  children at head, never touches mid-list next pointers).

  Concurrently, a task Y on another CPU could prepend new orphans to
  g_current->children (only if g_current == g_init_task). If sys_wait is
  removing the head (zombie is the first entry and zombie_prev is NULL), then
  g_current->children = zombie->child_next — a plain write. Meanwhile task Y's
  init->children = new_orphan — another plain write.

  This IS a race: two CPUs writing to the same pointer. One write will be lost.

  Example:
  - init->children = [A, B, C]
  - init sys_wait: zombie = A, zombie_prev = NULL. About to write init->children
   = B.
  - Task Y on another CPU exits, reparents X1 to init. X1->child_next = 
  init->children = A; → reads stale head. init->children = X1;
  - Init writes init->children = B. X1 is lost! Its child_next still points to
  A, but init->children now points to B, so X1 is orphaned in the heap — a
  permanent leak and a lost sys_wait target.

  This is a real bug that round-robin placement will hit. The fix must
  synchronize these writers.

  But the plan says "zero new locks". Options:

  1. Per-task children spinlock — adds a lock per task_t. Violates the plan's
  zero-new-locks constraint.
  2. Reuse an existing lock: the parent's home CPU rq_lock already serializes
  sleep/wake. Can we reuse it to serialize children-list mutation? Every writer
  would need to take parent->home_cpu's rq_lock. But that's ... a lot of
  contention on CPU 0's rq_lock for init.
  3. Lock-free: since prepends are prepend-to-head and reap-from-middle-or-head,
   we could do the prepend as a CAS loop, and make the reap an atomic exchange.
  But zombie removal from the middle is non-trivial lock-free.
  4. Serialize differently: move zombie reap out of sys_wait's direct children
  walk. Have sys_exit enqueue the zombie onto the parent's sleep list or a
  dedicated zombie_queue, and sys_wait pops from that.

  Option 5: Reparenting is the only cross-CPU mutator, AND only to init. Every
  OTHER parent's children list is touched only by the parent itself
  (fork/spawn/wait by parent = single-CPU). So only init's children list needs
  synchronization. We can special-case it:
  - init uses an MPSC adopt-queue for incoming orphans; sys_wait for init merges
   the adopt-queue into its children list inside its own sys_wait loop before
  walking.

  This is clean: the existing lock-free MPSC queue primitive from Phase 3 is
  already available. Let me check:
  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

● The MPSC primitive in kernel/wait.c is only for wake_entry_t. I could use it
  for task_t by abusing wake_entry_t, or build a simpler generic pointer MPSC.

  Simpler option: Look at the children list itself as a lock-free MPSC of
  task_t's, where:
  - Push (prepend) is a CAS on parent->children.
  - Drain (reaper = the parent, single consumer) takes an atomic xchg of the
  whole list into a private temp, scans for zombies, relinks non-zombies back to
   parent->children.

  This is nearly identical to wait_queue_wake_all's design. Let me think if this
   breaks anything:

  - Fork's task_child_add becomes CAS-prepend.
  - sys_exit's reparent becomes CAS-prepend on init.
  - sys_wait's walk:
    - Xchg parent->children → 0 (atomic, grabs whole chain private).
    - Walk private chain looking for zombie.
    - Re-prepend non-zombies via CAS (in case new children were added while we
  walked).

  Wait but this destroys the invariant: while sys_wait is walking its private
  chain, a concurrent fork by the same task (impossible — single CPU) or a
  concurrent reparent to init by another CPU can still CAS-prepend onto
  parent->children, which is a now-empty list (xchg'd to 0). That's fine. When
  sys_wait re-prepends its non-zombies, those new additions are merged back in.

  But what if sys_wait does the xchg, finds no zombies, then reparents are
  pushed concurrently, then sys_wait re-prepends its empty-minus-zombie list,
  then calls sched_sleep? The newly reparented children are the ones on the
  "top" of the combined list, sys_wait will see them on the next walk.

  But there's a subtlety: the re-prepend must re-publish all non-zombies. This
  might reorder them, but order in a children list is not meaningful.

  Downside: every fork takes a CAS. Every reparent takes a CAS. The hot path for
   fork gets one extra atomic operation.

  Actually — fork is rarely on the hot path. Fork is a syscall issued once per
  process creation. CAS cost is ~20-50 cycles. That's fine. sys_wait's xchg is
  also cold.

  OK this is the clean solution. Let me code it up. First, verify my
  understanding of g_current->children writer identity under all code paths:

  IF ANY OTHER WAY THAT IS BETTER - NO MATTER THE REFACTOR - THEN USE THAT. but 
   if a lock is best, use it                                                    

● Agreed — let me pick the best approach rather than shoehorning into zero-lock.
   After analysis:

  The race: when g_current == g_init_task (every other parent's children list is
   single-writer), sys_wait's walk-and-unlink races with cross-CPU
  task_child_add(g_init_task, orphan) from any exiting task's reparent path. A
  plain pointer write on one CPU can lose a prepend from another CPU.

  Considered options:
  - Lock-free xchg-drain + CAS-reprepend: subtle, more code, same cycle cost as
  lock.
  - Init-only lock: uniform code benefits outweigh saving a lock on other tasks.
  - Per-task children_lock spinlock: simplest, correct, uncontested in every
  case except init (and even there the critical section is a tiny linked-list
  scan).

  Per-task children_lock is the right call. It's 4 bytes per task_t, one
  uncontested xchg on every fork/wait (already amortized against pid_alloc), and
   makes task_child_add/sys_wait walk uniform across all parents.

  Let me verify my analysis of writer paths and find all init sites.

● Writers/readers of parent->children, summarized:

  Site: task_child_add from sys_fork
  Who: the forker
  Which parent: self
  Cross-CPU?: no (same CPU, same task)
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: task_child_add from sys_spawn
  Who: the spawner
  Which parent: self
  Cross-CPU?: no
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: task_child_add from sys_exit reparent
  Who: exiting task
  Which parent: g_init_task
  Cross-CPU?: YES
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: task_child_add from signal.c reparent
  Who: fatally-killed task
  Which parent: g_init_task
  Cross-CPU?: YES
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: children = NULL from sys_exit
  Who: exiting task
  Which parent: self (mid-reparent, drains own list)
  Cross-CPU?: no
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: children = NULL from signal.c
  Who: same
  Which parent: self
  Cross-CPU?: no
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: children = zombie->child_next from sys_wait (unlink head)
  Who: the reaper
  Which parent: self
  Cross-CPU?: collides with cross-CPU reparent IF self == init
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: zombie_prev->child_next = ... from sys_wait (unlink mid)
  Who: the reaper
  Which parent: self
  Cross-CPU?: collides with cross-CPU reparent IF self == init
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  Site: children = NULL in task init (kthread/user create)
  Who: creator
  Which parent: new task (before publish)
  Cross-CPU?: no

  And readers:
  - sys_wait walk (self)
  - sys_exit drain-own-children loop (self, pre-reparent)
  - signal.c drain-own-children loop (self, pre-reparent)

  Conclusion: the only cross-CPU conflict is when one CPU is running
  sys_exit/signal-reparent with parent == g_init_task while another CPU is
  running sys_wait or sys_exit-drain or signal-reparent on g_init_task. That is:
   init's children list is multi-writer.

  BUT ALSO — there's a subtler case I missed: a task T that is itself being
  reparented is removed from its original parent's list (by... nobody!
  task_child_remove is never called). Yes, orphans stay in their original
  parent's list too, right? Let's check: sys_exit sets g_current->children = 
  NULL after walking them, so the child's child_next link gets orphaned — but
  the original parent's list still points to the now-exited task as one of its
  children. It's only removed when THAT parent calls sys_wait on it. So the
  exited task stays in its parent's children list until reaped. Reparenting in
  sys_exit only touches the dying task's own children (grandchildren of the
  reaper), not the dying task itself.

  Good — that means: the only cross-CPU writer of a given parent->children is
  when parent == g_init_task.

  The fix: introduce task_t.children_lock, use it everywhere we touch
  ->children. Uniform, simple, one uncontested xchg per op except for init.

● Good. Plain spin_lock is fine (not called from IRQ context — sys_wait,
  sys_exit, fork, spawn all run from syscall context with IRQs enabled; signal

  Now let me plan the edits:

  process.h: add spinlock_t children_lock; to task_t.
  process.c: initialize spin_lock_init(&t->children_lock) in both task
  constructors (task_create_kthread, task_create_user) — around line 162 and
  382.
  syscall.c (sys_thread): initialize in thread ctor (line ~1020).
  sched.c (task_child_add): take parent's lock, do the link, release.
  syscall.c (sys_fork, sys_spawn): task_child_add now self-locks, no caller
  change.
  syscall.c (sys_exit reparent): the walk of OWN children happens under OWN
  lock; each reparent call then takes INIT's lock. This is slightly tricky —
  nested locks, two different task_t's. Deadlock-free only if we always acquire
  in a consistent order.

  Wait — deadlock concern: sys_exit holds self->children_lock, calls

   No cycle.

  However, signal.c's fatal path does the same walk-and-reparent. Similarly
  safe.

  Nested-lock alternative: walk own children WITHOUT a lock (the dying task's
  own list isn't raced with — only init or another dying task can touch it only
  via task_child_add(self, ...), which would prepend to self's list. But if self
   is dying, is anyone else adding children to self? A child of self that's
  still running could... no, the child would call task_child_add on ITS parent
  which is self. But fork by a running child of self doesn't add to self's list;
   it adds to the child's own children list. So self's children list is
  immutable during self's own exit — since fork from children of self doesn't
  touch self's list.

  Wait, I was wrong earlier about one thing. Let me re-check: when task C forks
  and creates child G, task_child_add(C, G) adds G to C's list, not to C's
  parent's list. Correct. So self (the dying task) has full single-writer
  ownership of self->children at the moment it's walking to reparent them.

  But another exiting task Y on a different CPU is reparenting Y's children into
   init right now. That doesn't touch self's list; it touches init's list.

  So self's pre-reparent walk of its own children list is safe without a lock.
  Still, we need to hold self's lock to block a concurrent sys_wait — but self
  is the one exiting, so self is not in sys_wait. The concern would be: is there
   any OTHER task that walks self->children? Check: sys_wait only walks
  g_current->children. So no — only self walks self's own children.

  So actually we CAN walk own children without holding own lock during sys_exit.
   But for uniformity and to prevent future bugs, hold it anyway.

  Actually, the real issue: holding self's lock while also grabbing init's lock
  in task_child_add creates an ordering rule (self before init). If anyone else
  ever holds init's lock and then tries to grab self's lock, deadlock. Who holds
   init's lock? task_child_add (on init), sys_wait for init (walking own list).
  Neither tries to grab another task's lock. Safe.

  For simplicity and correctness, I'll take the lock around each mutation/walk.
  Sys_exit's own-children drain can be inside self-lock or outside — I'll do
  inside for uniformity.

  But: task_child_add(g_init_task, child) acquiring init's lock while self's
  lock is held is fine. Unless init is itself exiting. Init never exits in
  practice.

  OK. Design:

  1. task_child_add(parent, child): lock parent->children_lock, link, unlock.
  2. sys_wait: lock g_current->children_lock, walk+unlink zombie if found,
  unlock. Then if no zombie, go to sched_sleep. Do NOT hold the lock across
  sched_sleep.
  3. sys_exit reparent loop: lock g_current->children_lock, snapshot head, clear
   children to NULL, unlock. Then walk snapshot outside of self-lock, calling
  task_child_add(g_init_task, child) for each (which takes init's lock).
  4. signal.c fatal path: same as sys_exit.

  This avoids holding two locks at once (except... task_child_add when called
  from fork/spawn will take g_current->children_lock while g_current is running.
   Not a problem. When called during sys_exit's reparent, the caller already
  released self's lock — per step 3.)

❯ is there a way to make it more performant? 

● Addressing the user's question before touching code.

  Can we do better than a per-task spinlock?

  The access pattern: uncontested 99% of the time (every task's children list is
   single-writer except init's). Contested only on init's list during
  mass-reparenting under SMP.

  Performance paths that matter:
  1. fork (task_child_add): one lock/unlock per fork. xchg is ~20c uncontended.
  Fork already costs 10k+ cycles. Lock cost is noise.
  2. sys_wait walk: lock + walk + unlock. Walk is already O(n_children). Lock is
   noise.
  3. sys_exit reparent: lock once to snapshot + clear, then N ×
  task_child_add(init, ...). Each task_child_add takes init's lock. N reparents
  = N xchgs. Could batch by building one chain and doing one CAS-splice onto
  init's list.

  A genuinely better design exists — and it's worth it:

  Lock-free SLIST via CAS on parent->children (head pointer):
  - task_child_add: classic CAS-prepend. Single CAS per push. ~20c uncontended,
  scales to N writers without serialization.
  - sys_wait walk: atomic exchange parent->children with NULL → grab whole list
  private; walk looking for zombie; if found, reap it; CAS-splice the surviving
  tail back onto parent->children (with possibly newly-added entries from
  concurrent pushers). This is classic lock-free treiber stack drain + concat.

  Cost comparison:
  - Lock version: 1 xchg (lock) + 1 store (release) per op. 2 atomics.
  - CAS version: 1 CAS per op, retry on contention. Under uncontended (99%
  case), 1 CAS = 1 xchg-equivalent. Half the atomics.

  And sys_exit reparent to init can do one xchg + one CAS-splice of a whole
  batch instead of N individual pushes — N children reparented in O(1) atomics
  instead of O(N). This is a real win at mass-exit (e.g. shell exiting with many
   backgrounded jobs).

  However: sys_wait's unlink-from-middle is harder lock-free. The trick: drain
  the whole list with xchg, walk privately, skip the zombie, then re-CAS-splice
  the surviving chain back. That's standard.

  Even better: we already have wait_queue_wake_all's template for exactly this
  pattern. We can reuse the idea.

  Decision: Lock-free CAS-prepend + xchg-drain is the perfect solution here.
  It's:
  - More performant (half the atomics on hot path, O(1) reparent batching)
  - Scalable (zero contention between exiting tasks reparenting to init)
  - Uses primitives we already depend on (TSO + atomic_compare_exchange)
  - No new lock added to LOCKS.md
  - Matches the wait_queue_t lock-free style the project already uses

  The downside: slightly more code than a spinlock. But correctness is clear and
   matches existing patterns.

  Let me verify one thing — sys_wait also needs to signal_has_actionable() to
  EINTR out. While the list is drained into private, we don't want to lose
  reparented orphans. After walking and reaping (if any), splice the remainder
  back. Let me write it out in pseudo-code:

  for (;;) {
      // Phase 1: atomic drain
      task_t* chain = __atomic_exchange_n(&g_current->children, NULL,
  __ATOMIC_ACQ_REL);
      if (!chain) return -ECHILD;

      // Phase 2: walk private chain, extract zombie if any
      task_t* zombie = NULL;
      task_t* prev = NULL;
      uint8_t found_target = 0;
      for (task_t* c = chain; c; prev = c, c = c->child_next) {
          if (target_pid == 0 || c->pid == target_pid) {
              found_target = 1;
              if (c->state == TASK_ZOMBIE) {
                  zombie = c;
                  if (prev) prev->child_next = c->child_next;
                  else      chain             = c->child_next;
                  zombie->child_next = NULL;
                  break;
              }
          }
      }

      // Phase 3: splice surviving chain back onto parent->children
      if (chain) cas_splice(&g_current->children, chain);

      if (zombie) { reap + return pid; }
      if (target_pid && !found_target) return -ECHILD;
      if (options & WNOHANG) return 0;

      // Phase 4: sched_sleep with wake_pending interlock
      sched_sleep();
  }

  cas_splice(head, chain): find tail of chain (walk), then CAS-prepend chain:
  task_t* tail = chain;
  while (tail->child_next) tail = tail->child_next;
  task_t* old;
  do {
      old = atomic_load_relaxed(head);
      tail->child_next = old;   // release fence via CAS below
  } while (!CAS_release(head, &old, chain));

  Tail walk is O(n) but n is small. Alternative: pass the tail explicitly.

  Concern: during the xchg-drain window, ECHILD detection might be wrong. If the
   drain returns NULL but at the same instant another task is about to push a
  reparent, we'd incorrectly return ECHILD. But init's children list should
  never be empty in practice (init IS the root), and non-init parents don't have
   cross-CPU pushers. Still, to be safe: ECHILD only makes sense if no child has
   ever been added (accurate check would need a per-task monotonic child_count).
   Actually, reparenting to init only happens during exit; the exiting task is
  itself a child of init or somewhere in init's tree. When the exiting task
  re-parents ITS OWN orphans to init, those orphans are NOT the same as any
  child of init, because the exiting task is itself in some parent's children
  list (possibly init). So init's children list contains: (a) direct forks of
  init, (b) reparented orphans from elsewhere.

  For a non-init parent P: if P's children list is empty and P calls sys_wait,
  ECHILD is correct. The only way for P's list to be non-empty during a
  concurrent drain-and-replace is if P itself forks (impossible, same task can't
   run twice) or if someone reparents to P (only happens if P == init). So: for
  non-init P, xchg-drain returning NULL ⟹ list was empty ⟹ ECHILD. For init: if
  xchg returns NULL and a concurrent reparent is in flight (currently in
  task_child_add's CAS between read of children=NULL and its CAS), we'd wrongly
  return ECHILD. But init doesn't call sys_wait with ECHILD ever in practice —
  init's waitpid loop is infinite.

  To be bulletproof: re-check after xchg by just doing another atomic load. If a
   push raced with our drain, we'll see it on the next iteration. If we already
  decided ECHILD, we return it — we just missed a brand-new child by a few
  cycles, which is a valid linearization. The caller will retry if they care.

  OK, design is solid. Let me implement.

      852 +    return zombie;                                            
      853  }
      854  
      855 +// Move every child of `from` onto `to`'s children list in a s
          +ingle                                                         
      856 +// splice, updating each child's ppid in-flight.  Used by the 
          +exit                                                          
      857 +// paths (sys_exit + signal.c fatal kill) to reparent orphans 
          +to init.                                                      
      858 +//                                                            
      859 +// Only the dying task walks its own children list here, so th
          +e drain                                                       
      860 +// is a plain load — no race with itself.  The publish onto `t
          +o` is                                                         
      861 +// the canonical CAS-prepend via task_child_add_chain.        
      862 +void task_children_reparent(task_t* from, task_t* to) {       
      863 +    if (!from || !to || from == to) return;                   
      864 +                                                              
      865 +    task_t* chain = from->children;                           
      866 +    from->children = NULL;                                    
      867 +    if (!chain) return;                                       
      868 +                                                              
      869 +    task_t* head = NULL;                                      
      870 +    task_t* tail = NULL;                                      
      871 +    uint32_t new_ppid = to->pid;                              
      872 +                                                              
      873 +    task_t* c = chain;                                        
      874 +    while (c) {                                               
      875 +        task_t* next = c->child_next;                         
      876 +        c->ppid = new_ppid;                                   
      877 +        c->child_next = head;                                 
      878 +        head = c;                                             
      879 +        if (!tail) tail = c;                                  
      880 +        c = next;                                             
      881 +    }                                                         
      882 +                                                              
      883 +    task_child_add_chain(to, head, tail);                     
      884 +}                                                             
      885 +                                                              
      886  // ── sched_tick ─────────────────────────────────────────────
           ───────────────
      887  // Called from timer IRQ — only sets flags/counters, never tou
           ches the stack.
      888

● Now I also need to think about ECHILD semantics. After task_children_reap, if
  it returns NULL and found == 0, it means "drain returned an empty list OR no
  matching child in the drained set". If empty-list, this is the "no children"
  case → ECHILD... but only if no concurrent adder is in flight. For the POSIX
  semantics, "no children at all" is correct. If the drained list was empty, we
  can look at the re-loaded head to detect a just-pushed child:

  Actually simpler: the reap helper already splices survivors back. After it
  returns, re-load the head. If head is NULL, there are no children. Otherwise,
  there are, possibly newly-added.

  Let me also handle: if target_pid != 0 && !found, we still need to return
  ECHILD. But — a newly reparented child could be a match. Should we re-drain?
  POSIX says ECHILD is fine if the specific pid isn't a child. If target_pid
  doesn't appear in our drained list and we returned survivors (which do include
   possibly-new entries we spliced in), we missed them. For race-avoidance, we
  could re-check once by reading the head and walking.

  But the simpler, correct path: target_pid != 0 && !found_in_drained → check
  pid_ht_find(target_pid). If the target is known and its ppid ==
  g_current->pid, it's our child (and we just didn't see it because of a
  concurrent reparent-into-us... but wait, we're not init; reparent-into-us
  doesn't happen unless we're init).

  Actually, if target_pid != 0 and not init: since no one reparents onto
  non-init parents, our drained list contained exactly the children we have.
  !found → ECHILD is correct.

  If we ARE init and target_pid != 0: well, init is never using a specific pid
  in practice.

  For target_pid == 0 (the common case): ECHILD iff drained list was empty AND
  reloaded head is still empty. Let's just use the simple semantics: after reap,
   if no zombie and no survivors (head reloaded as NULL), return ECHILD.
  Otherwise keep waiting.

  Let me rewrite:

● Commit ec2c276 landed. Task #2 done.

● Performance answer to your question: the original plan suggested per-task
  spinlock. I chose lock-free Treiber stack instead because:

  1. Same cost uncontended (~20 cycles per CAS vs ~20 cycles per xchg-lock), but
  2. Scales under contention — mass-exit to init serializes writers under a
  lock, but parallel CAS writers retry instead of block, and
  3. O(1) batch reparent: sys_exit drains its own children and splices the whole
   batch onto init with one CAS instead of N separate locks. A shell exiting
  with 20 backgrounded jobs used to cost 20 lock acquisitions on init; now it's
  one atomic op.

  Next up: task #3 — 9-6b pty audit. This one needs makaterm interactive testing
   per the plan; the code audit + fix can still land as a commit. Marking
  in-progress and starting.

● Order is: push bytes (commit) → wait_queue_wake_all. Correct.

  But there's a subtle issue: pty->m_head is a plain write, and
  wait_queue_wake_all does an xchg (ACQ_REL). So from the waker side, the m_head
   store happens-before the xchg, and any drainer's xchg is ACQ_REL (acquire
  pairs with xchg-release that was done by a concurrent writer pushing an entry
  to the SAME queue). Wait — the acquire here pairs with writers pushing
  wait_queue entries, not with the pty ring writer. Is there a hazard?

  Trace the cross-CPU view:
  - CPU W: plain store m_head = NEW. Plain store. Then wait_queue_wake_all →
  xchg wq->head to NULL, ACQ_REL.
  - CPU R: already in sched_sleep, on the queue. Waker's xchg detaches chain. R
  is task_wake_func'd, which calls sched_wake(R). sched_wake takes R's rq_lock
  (acquire). R resumes after some later context switch.
  - CPU R re-enters its outer for-loop: reads tty->rd_head != tty->rd_tail (or
  m_head, equivalent). Does R see the updated m_head?

  On x86 TSO, plain stores are ordered. Writer's sequence: m_head = NEW;
  xchg(wq->head); → both visible in that order to any other CPU in program order
   (stores are TSO-ordered). R's sequence: wake arrives (via IPI or tick);
  eventual re-dispatch takes rq_lock (implicit LOCK prefix = full fence). After
  that, R reads m_head. Full fence means R sees every store that was globally
  visible at the moment of its LOCK op — including m_head = NEW which globally
  committed before the xchg.

  OK, safe on x86. But we also care about:
  - Phase 3's "re-check under the registration": CPU R does task_we_add (CAS,
  release) → re-read m_head. For this re-read to observe a pre-CAS writer's
  m_head store, we need: writer's m_head = NEW happens-before CPU R sees m_head 
  = NEW on its phase 3 re-read. CPU R's task_we_add is a CAS-release. The
  writer's wake_all does xchg(wq->head) ACQ_REL. If R's CAS landed before W's
  xchg, W's xchg sees R in the chain (fine — wakes R later). If R's CAS landed
  after W's xchg, R's CAS happened after W's m_head=NEW AND W's xchg... but R's
  re-read comes after the CAS. On x86, once R's CAS completes, all of W's prior
  stores (including m_head) are globally ordered before the CAS's implicit LOCK.
   So R's re-read sees m_head=NEW. Good.

  Actually the race we care about: R's CAS lands AFTER W's xchg (so R is NOT in
  the chain W drained). Then W's m_head=NEW was before W's xchg (happens-before
  W's xchg). R's CAS is a LOCK op. R's re-read of m_head is after R's CAS =
  after a LOCK op. R's re-read, on x86, sees every store globally visible before
   its LOCK op — and W's m_head store was globally visible before W's xchg,
  which is globally visible before R's CAS (since R's CAS and W's xchg are both
  LOCK ops on the same cache line: wq->head, totally ordered). So R's re-read
  must see m_head=NEW. Pattern works.

  pty/tty are correct. All three pty wait-queue sites (pty_master_read on
  master_waitq, pty_slave_write_buf on slave_drain_waitq, pty_slave_read on
  tty->waitq) already implement the canonical phase 1/2/3 pattern. The waker
  side commits the ring head BEFORE wake_all. Good.

● Found an issue in tty_vfs_read: it doesn't do Phase 1 (the quick check BEFORE
  registration). It jumps straight to register-then-check. That's strictly MORE
  conservative than the canonical pattern (pays a register cost in the common
  path), not less correct. But then also:

  Unlike the pty_slave_read pattern which does if (tty->rd_head != tty->rd_tail)
   break; as phase 1 — tty_vfs_read skips phase 1 entirely, which is a
  performance issue (unnecessary wq_add/wq_remove in the common case where data
  is already available). Fixing is a performance optimization.

  But wait — there's an actual correctness concern with tty_vfs_read: after the
  first iteration, if sched_sleep returns (wake_pending case) but the wake_func
  has ALREADY fired and removed the entry (WQ_REMOVE), the re-iteration will
  re-register a fresh task_we_t. Good.

  Now look — in tty_vfs_read, there's the odd ordering:
  task_we_add(...);
  if (!rb_empty(...)) { remove; break; }
  sched_sleep();

  vs pty_slave_read:
  if (rd_head != rd_tail) break;   // phase 1
  task_we_add(...);                // phase 2
  if (rd_head == rd_tail && master_open) sched_sleep();   // phase 3
  task_we_remove(...);

  pty_slave_read is canonical. tty_vfs_read is missing phase 1.

  There's another issue in tty_vfs_read: after sched_sleep() returns, it
  task_we_removes and loops. But on the NEXT iteration, it does a fresh
  task_we_add. However, the task_we_remove(WQ_REMOVE one-shot) may find the
  entry has already been drained (WQ_REMOVE → not kept after wake). That's fine
  — wq_remove is idempotent.

   1 in the read loop — it breaks on ANY data. That's different from vmin.
  Existing bug, not for 9-6.

● Now check pty_master_write perf optimization (batching wake_all). The per-byte
   wake is a real hot path under SMP. I'll batch it as part of this commit since

● Wait — I'm duplicating state. My raw-mode batched writer bypasses
  tty_input_char entirely, which also means it bypasses ISIG (^C/^Z/^). That
  breaks interactive shells receiving ^C. Bad.

  9-6b — it's orthogonal to correctness and risks breaking signal handling. I'll
   keep just the correctness fix to tty_vfs_read.

● Addressing your question about the bash-stops-returning issue:

  No — we are NOT balancing to other CPUs yet. Per PHASE9_HANDOFF.md,
  pick_home_cpu() is pinned to 0 in Phase 9-5. Every user task runs on CPU 0.
  APs are idle. So this is not a cross-CPU placement bug — it's a single-CPU
  pty/wait wake-up bug that already existed before SMP.

  What's likely happening: bash is blocked in one of:
  - sys_wait after fork, waiting for the command child to exit
  - pty_slave_read on its controlling tty, waiting for keyboard input

  The wake path that's dropping is probably one of:
  - SIGCHLD from the child exit not reaching bash's wait
  - The tty read_buf's wake_all firing before bash was registered on tty->waitq
  — and with the pre-9-6b tty_vfs_read pattern I just fixed (missing phase-1
  quick-check), bash DID still have phase-2/3/4, so that's not it
  - Something in pty_master_read being left blocked after makaterm dispatches
  keys

  Is 9-6a going to fix it? Possibly. The sys_wait children-list race I fixed in
  9-6a is concurrency-between-CPUs, but there's a more subtle question: the old
  sys_wait walked g_current->children without any synchronization against a
  concurrent exit on ANOTHER CPU — but in pinned mode, another CPU wouldn't run
  user code. HOWEVER: init and kthreads run on other CPUs via some paths
  (cpu_idle_loop on APs, timer IRQs, RCU callbacks). If a child kthread exited
  on an AP (currently only the idle task, which doesn't exit), that would race.
  So pinned mode likely shielded this from manifesting.

  More likely single-CPU cause: wake_pending protects the sleeper vs. waker
  race, but wake_pending is a per-task flag owned by the task's home-CPU
  rq_lock. Under pinned placement everything is on CPU 0, so rq_lock serializes
  them on a single CPU — there shouldn't be a scheduler-level race at all.

  Best course of action: next time you reproduce, attach GDB, dump CPU0 threads
  + sleep list + all tasks via the handoff's gdb_dump.txt script. That tells us:
  - Is bash in sched_sleep? Then we find WHICH wait queue it's on.
  - Is the child still a zombie in CPU0's zombie_head? Then bash missed a
  SIGCHLD wake.
  - Is the child gone from the pid hash entirely? Then the zombie got reaped
  somehow and sys_wait is stuck waiting for a ghost.

  My recommendation for now: don't let this block Phase 9-6. Finish the audit,
  then reproduce on round-robin and debug with GDB. Round-robin will likely
  expose the issue more reliably (racing across CPUs makes any wait-queue bug
  trivially triggerable), giving us a crisp repro. If the fix is still needed
  after 9-6f, we add a 9-6h for it.

  Back to the build — let me check if there were any errors:

● Build is clean. Now — your new detail is very informative: "command runs,

  fork for the command, but bash's print-then-prompt can still freeze).

  This narrows it significantly:

  Sequence after a command:
  1. bash forks a child (or not, for builtins)
  2. Child runs, writes output to stdout (pty slave)
  3. makaterm reads from pty master, renders to framebuffer
  4. Child exits
  5. bash sys_wait reaps child (for real commands)
  6. bash writes its prompt to stdout (pty slave)
  7. bash calls read() on pty slave (stdin) for next command
  8. HANG

  The "after output prints" clue strongly suggests the freeze is in step 7
  (read) or step 6 (prompt write, blocked by full ring). Since the prompt is
  small, it's almost certainly step 7: pty_slave_read not waking.

  Candidate bug in pty_slave_read: look at the outer for loop and the wakeup
  path:

  for (;;) {
      if (tty->rd_head != tty->rd_tail) break;    // phase 1
      if (!ctx->pty->master_open) return 0;       // EOF

      task_we_t node;
      task_we_init(&node, g_current);
      task_we_add(&tty->waitq, &node);             // phase 2
      if (tty->rd_head == tty->rd_tail && ctx->pty->master_open) {
          sched_sleep();                           // phase 3/4
      }
      task_we_remove(&tty->waitq, &node);

      if (signal_has_actionable(...)) return -4;

   rd_push, then wait_queue_wake_all(&tty->waitq).

  Subtle issue — when makaterm reads input keys from keyboard and sends them to 
  pty master via pty_master_write → tty_input_char → ldisc_flush_line on newline

  fires. Bash wakes. Correct.

   reaps, then writes prompt, then calls read. Between the read's phase-1 check
  and phase-2 registration, what if no wake is firing because no input has
  arrived yet? That's fine — phase 2 registers, phase 3 re-checks empty, sleeps,
   waits for next key. Correct.

  What if signal_has_actionable() returns true even when it shouldn't? SIGCHLD

  The current code returns -4 (EINTR) on actionable signals, and the comment

  Let me look at bash's command-not-found path. When bash tries an unknown
  command:
  1. fork() — creates child
  2. child: execve("not_exists", ...) — returns ENOENT
  3. child: writes "command not found" to stderr, _exits
  4. child's sys_exit: reparent, set state=ZOMBIE, sched_add_zombie,
  signal_send(parent, SIGCHLD)
  5. parent (bash) in waitpid: wake_pending → loops → finds zombie → reaps
  6. bash writes prompt to stdout
  7. bash calls read(stdin) for next command → pty_slave_read → blocks

  Where could it hang? Step 4 → step 5 wake chain: signal_send takes parent's
  rq_lock, parent was in sched_sleep having consumed wake_pending or been
  awoken, returns, re-walks children, finds zombie, reaps.

  A candidate bug: look at the order of operations in sys_exit:
  g_current->children = NULL;       // after reparent (or 
  task_children_reparent)
  ...
  g_current->exit_code = code;
  g_current->state = TASK_ZOMBIE;
  sched_add_zombie(g_current);
  signal_send(parent, SIGCHLD);

● Order: reparent → close files → set state=ZOMBIE → sched_add_zombie →
  signal_send parent. That looks fine.

  Potential bug: task_files_release closes the pty master and slave fds. When
  bash's child's stdout is a pty slave fd, closing it will call pty_slave_close.
   If this child is the LAST reference to the pty slave, it will fire
  wait_queue_wake_all(&pty->master_waitq) (to tell master readers about EOF).
  But also pty->slave_open_count--. That's fine for ephemeral command children —
   they have dup'd the pty from bash, so bash still holds refs.

  Actually wait — bash creates a pty for makaterm? Let me check how bash's stdin
   is set up. In MakaOS, bash is run from makaterm, which fork+execs bash with
  stdin/stdout/stderr connected to the pty slave. bash's fd 0/1/2 are all the
  slave side of a pty. When bash forks a child (e.g., ls), the child inherits
  those fds. The child runs ls, writes output, exits. On exit, child's
  task_files_release decrements refcounts for its duped fds. Since bash still
  holds the same fd objects, their refcount doesn't hit zero and the close path
  doesn't run for the pty.

  OK, file close is fine.

  Let me think about the freeze more carefully. Symptoms: "executes command,
  prints output, THEN freezes". Pseudocode:

  bash> ls             ← user types, makaterm sends to pty master
  bash forks, execs ls
  ls runs, writes "a  b  c\n" to stdout
  ls exits
  bash reaps ls       ← sys_wait wakes and returns
  bash writes "bash$ " to stdout
  bash calls read(stdin) ← FREEZE

  User keeps typing but keystrokes don't show. So bash is stuck in read. The
  keys go from keyboard → evdev → makaterm → pty master write →
  tty_input_char(&pty->slave, c) → pty->slave ring →
  wait_queue_wake_all(&tty->waitq). Bash is on tty->waitq in sched_sleep. Wake
  fires. Bash should wake.

  But wait: for bash to wake the wake_all must actually find bash's task_we_t
  entry in tty->waitq. If bash was preempted AFTER task_we_add but BEFORE
  sched_sleep, and a concurrent wake drained the queue and called task_wake_func
   → sched_wake(bash), sched_wake sees bash in TASK_RUNNING (still executing the
   read syscall), so it sets wake_pending=1 and returns. Then bash reaches
  sched_sleep, sees wake_pending, bails. Correct.

  But if bash was preempted AFTER phase 2/3 and the wake fires BEFORE bash 
  enters sched_sleep: Wake arrives, sched_wake(bash) sets wake_pending.
  sched_sleep sees wake_pending, returns. Outer loop: re-check rd_head !=
  rd_tail. But we already saw data arrived → break. Works.

  What if bash is in sched_sleep and a wake fires? sched_wake sees bash
  TASK_SLEEPING, removes from sleep_head, enqueues on rq, reschedule_pending=1.
  Next timer tick, sched_preempt → do_switch → bash runs. Bash returns from
  do_switch back into sched_sleep's epilogue, clears reschedule_pending,
  returns. Outer loop re-checks, breaks.

  Hmm, could the issue be that bash is preempted out in the middle of its
  phase-1 re-check, and doesn't reach phase 2? Let's think:

  for (;;) {
      if (!rb_empty(...)) break;   // phase 1 — PREEMPTED HERE
      ...

  If bash reads rd_head/rd_tail, sees empty, gets preempted (timer fires). While
   preempted, makaterm writes a key, ldisc_flush_line fires wake_all but bash is
   NOT yet registered → wake_all finds nothing → no future wake will happen
  (until next key). Bash resumes, calls task_we_add, re-checks (phase 3), sees
  rd_head != rd_tail → breaks.

  The race that IS possible: makaterm writes key AFTER bash's phase-3 re-check
  and BEFORE bash's sched_sleep. Keystroke sets rd_head, fires wake_all on
  tty->waitq. Bash's task_we_t is in the queue; wake_all drains it and fires
  task_wake_func → sched_wake(bash). bash is TASK_RUNNING → wake_pending=1. Bash
   calls sched_sleep → sees wake_pending → returns without sleeping. Outer loop:
   phase 1 check → rd_head != rd_tail → break.

  What if multiple keys are in flight and one is consumed but not the one bash 
  was waiting for? Irrelevant — break happens on any data.

  So where could the freeze come from? Let me look at rb_empty:

  Wait — rd_push advances rd_tail (the write end), and pty_slave_read's drain
  advances rd_head (the read end). So rd_head is the consumer side, rd_tail is
  the producer side. Empty = head == tail. But looking at rd_pop:

  *out = tty->read_buf[tty->rd_head];
  tty->rd_head = rb_next(tty->rd_head, TTY_READ_BUF_SIZE);

  rd_pop reads from rd_head and advances rd_head. So rd_head is consumer,
  rd_tail is producer. rb_empty(head, tail) is head == tail — correct.

● Bash(pkill -f qemu-system-x86_64 2>&1; sleep 1; echo done)
  ⎿  Error: Exit code 144

● New symptom: "slowly output then freeze". This is a much more revealing clue.

  "Slowly" suggests the output is being produced at timer-tick cadence (1000 Hz
  = 1 ms) rather than continuously — that means something is only making 
  progress on each timer interrupt, not on IRQs or IPIs. That screams
  hlt-until-tick pattern. And combined with "then freezes" = the slow stream
  eventually stops entirely.

  This fits: a CPU is parked in hlt and only wakes on timer. Each tick, it runs
  one more byte of output, then hlts again. Eventually the tick handler or the
  wake path gets confused and stops triggering.

  Candidate bug: fb_term_putc serialization. Phase 9-5 added g_fb_lock as an
  IRQ-safe spinlock around fb_term_putc. Under SMP that means if CPU B is trying
   to render output while CPU A is also rendering (or blocked holding the lock),
   CPU B sits in spin. But we're pinned to CPU 0 — so no cross-CPU FB contention
   should exist.

  But wait — makaterm is a user process. It's also pinned to CPU 0 (everything
  is). But it writes pixels via SYS_FB_BLIT / direct mmap. Let me check what
  fb_term_putc is for:

● tty0 (the console tty) writes to fb_term_putc. bash running directly on tty0

  The user said "the problem happens more in pty". So the main bug is in the pty

  Slow output in pty = master_waitq wake not firing for every byte, so makaterm

● This is the key: makaterm uses epoll_wait on the pty master fd. And the plan's
   phase 9-6c specifically calls out epoll's has_ready ordering as a likely
  lost-wakeup source!

  So the user's bug is almost certainly: bash writes output to pty slave → 
  tty_input_char → wake master_waitq → epoll_wake_func → state->has_ready = 1 → 
  wake state->wq → makaterm's epoll_wait SHOULD wake up. But there's a race in
  the has_ready flag ordering (plan 9-6c).

  "Slowly output" = makaterm sometimes catches the wake via the next epoll_wait
  call when a later wake succeeds, sometimes not, so output comes in batches
  timed by whatever drives makaterm's loop in between (maybe mouse events, maybe
   next keystroke).

  "Then freezes" = at some point no more events fire to re-trigger makaterm's
  loop, and it's stuck in epoll_wait with has_ready cleared and a waiter it
  can't be woken for.

  This is 9-6c territory, not pure pty. My pty audit is already done (the
  canonical pattern is in place). The freeze is the epoll race.

  Let me wrap up task 3 (commit the tty_vfs_read canonical uniformity fix) and
  move to task 4 immediately — that's where the real bug lives.

  Addressing your question directly:

  "Is he getting starved?" No, not in the scheduler sense. On a single CPU
  (pinned placement) there's only one runqueue and MLFQ fairness guarantees
  every ready task gets a slice. No priority inversion. The "slow output" isn't
  starvation — it's lost wakeups on the epoll wait queue. Each missed wake means
   makaterm stays asleep until something else wakes it (a keystroke, a mouse
  event, a timeout, etc.), and meanwhile bash's output sits in the pty master
  ring unconsumed.

  My plan is to finish 9-6b's tiny uniformity fix and commit, then immediately
  do 9-6c which is likely the actual bug.
