● Found the exact moment of failure. Timeline (pid 0x14=20 makaterm, pid 0x15=21
   bash, pid 0x4C=76 some child):

  1. makaterm writes keys to pty, bash (21) wakes and reads them — cycle
  working.
  2. slave_write_buf.wake_master pid=0x4C pushed=… — bash's CHILD pid 76 writes
  output (probably "bash: foo: command not found\n").
  3. [exit] pid=0x4C — child 76 exits with code (missing from trace).
  4. makaterm does master_read.enter → master_read.return — reads the output.
  Good.
  5. slave_write_buf.wake_master pid=0x15 — bash (pid 21) writes output (next
  prompt, 27 bytes probably).
  6. slave_read.registered pid=0x15 — bash registers on tty->waitq (the 9-6b
  canonical pattern — add to queue before re-check).
  7. slave_read.sleep pid=0x15 — bash enters sched_sleep.
  8. makaterm does master_read.enter → master_read.return — reads bash's prompt
  output.
  9. FREEZE — only ldisc_flush_line len=0 from tty0 keyboard input onwards.

  The bug: between bash's slave_write_buf.wake_master (step 5) and
  slave_read.sleep (step 7), bash OUTPUTS its prompt to the pty master then
  immediately goes to sleep waiting for input. Makaterm reads the prompt (step
  8). Now makaterm needs to wait for the next user key. makaterm goes back to
  epoll_wait (its own sleep).

  And now nothing wakes anyone. Keys pressed by you go to tty0 (not the pty),
  and ldisc_flush_line fires on tty0 which wakes tty0's bash (pid 18) — not
  makaterm.

  So makaterm is supposed to wake from a UNIX socket read (keys from 
  makadisplay). Every key should come evdev → makadisplay → unix socket →
  makaterm → pty master write. But we see no pty_master_write after the freeze —
   meaning makaterm never wakes. Which means makadisplay never forwards the key.
   Which means makadisplay never wakes on the evdev event.

  So the lost wake is at the evdev → makadisplay step.

  Let me check: does evdev_on_event actually push events when keys arrive? If
  s_sc_head moves (from the kbd ISR) but evdev ring doesn't, the kbd kthread
  isn't running or isn't draining. Or if evdev ring moves but no wake fires, the
   wait_queue_wake_all is broken.

  Let me add a trace to evdev_on_event and to the wake_all call there so we can
  see if keystrokes reach evdev during the freeze.

   (pid 19) is NOT waking, even though wait_queue_wake_all is being called on
  its evdev file waitq. The wake is being dispatched to the wait queue but the 
  epoll_wake_func → state->wq wake → task_wake_func → sched_wake chain isn't 
  delivering.

  This is an epoll wake chain lost wake. Let me look: the evdev wait_queue has
  one epoll_we_t (from makadisplay). wait_queue_wake_all detaches the chain,
  walks it, calls chain->func(chain) which is epoll_wake_func. epoll_wake_func
  sets *ewe->p_has_ready = 1 (atomic release), then
  wait_queue_wake_all(ewe->epoll_wq) (= &state->wq). That wake_all drains
  state->wq and wakes any task_we_t on it.

  But state->wq might be empty at the moment of wake because makadisplay is
  between task_we_add and sched_sleep in sys_epoll_wait — OR because makadisplay
   is already running the drain loop.

  attach gdb. Let me look at this from the trace alone.

❯ im not able to reproduce the crash even after running it 5 times. did you     
  even make any code changes in the last commit? also i feel like everything    
  got slower (especially pty) tho. did you ALWAYS implement the PERFECT, most   
  PERFORMANT - ONLY LOCK WHERE ACTUALLY NEEDED solution? did you add expedited  
  rcu?                                                                          

● Honest answer: no, the performance is NOT optimal, and I made mistakes.

  Problems with what I did:

  1. The serial tracing is killing your pty performance. Every PTT(...),
  [evdev], [ewake], [epwait], [hb], [sigsend], [fatal] call takes the global
  serial lock and does port I/O at ~10KB/s. On the pty hot path that's dozens of
   locked serial writes per keystroke. That's why "everything got slower."
  2. Our call_rcu is synchronous. It does synchronize_rcu(); func(data); —
  meaning every kfree_rcu() call blocks the current task until every CPU passes
  a quiescent state. Every file close now stalls for potentially milliseconds.
  No, I did NOT add expedited RCU. The proper solution is truly deferred
  callbacks (queue the free, process later on a timer or grace-period
  completion). We don't have that infrastructure.
  3. The crash probably stopped reproducing because of the per-CPU syscall 
  scratch fix (commit 13609e1), not the tracing or RCU changes. That was the
  real master bug — global syscall scratch racing between CPUs was corrupting
  signal delivery, exec state, and user register restoration. The freeze was
  likely a symptom of that corruption.

  What needs to happen:

  1. Strip ALL debug tracing — PTY_TRACE, evdev trace, heartbeat, sigsend/fatal
  trace. They served their purpose.
  2. Revert kfree_rcu back to kfree everywhere EXCEPT epoll_watch_t (the one
  proven UAF). For that one, use a proper deferred free — or better, just ensure
   the close path waits out any drainer by calling synchronize_rcu() ONCE at the
   top of epoll_close, then kfree everything directly after.
  3. Remove PMM_DEBUG_ALWAYS_ZERO — it was a diagnostic tool.

  Want me to do a cleanup commit that strips all the debug overhead and makes
  the RCU usage surgical (only where proven needed)?

● Now issue 2: Ctrl+C in pty bash causes #GP. Let me understand what happens on
  Ctrl+C:

  1. You type Ctrl+C in makaterm window.
  2. makadisplay captures it, sends to makaterm via socket.
  3. makaterm writes Ctrl+C (0x03) to pty master.
  4. tty_input_char sees it, sees ISIG + VINTR → sends SIGINT to tty->fg_pgid
  (the pty's foreground pgroup = bash and its current child command).
  5. signal_send_pgrp(tty->fg_pgid, SIGINT) — walks the pgrp hash.
  6. Each target gets SIGINT. SIG_DFL for SIGINT is terminate.
  7. bash is typically in sys_wait (waiting for its child). Child is in some
  syscall.

  The #GP must come from signal delivery into one of these tasks. Let me check
  what signal_send_pgrp does and where a #GP could come from:

  Searched for 1 pattern (ctrl+o to expand)

  What corrupts the kernel stack between line 67 push and line 90 pop?

  That window is when call syscall_dispatch runs. Syscall_dispatch pushes its
  own frame, calls the handler, unwinds. If syscall_dispatch's handler CORRUPTS
  THE STACK above our push, the pop reads garbage.

  Possibilities:
  - C code writing past a local buffer into higher stack addresses (toward the
  pushed values).

  somehow the kernel stack.

  Actually wait — on syscall entry we switch to [gs:CPU_TSS_RSP0] which is
  kstack_top for the CURRENT task. The kstack is per-task, not per-CPU. Each
  task has its own 8 KB kstack. The pushed rcx/r11/rsp values are at the top of
  the current task's kstack.

  If the task's kstack gets REUSED or CORRUPTED by something else during
  syscall_dispatch, pop reads garbage.

  Suspect: context_switch inside sched_sleep. If the task sleeps during
  syscall_dispatch, it context-switches away. On return (wake), it
  context-switches back. That happens on the task's own kstack. The saved
  rcx/r11/rsp at the top of the kstack should be preserved across context_switch
   — because pop rsp will restore them.

  Actually context_switch saves/restores rsp AND the saved GPRs. The
  pushed-to-kstack user registers (at offset ~32 above the current rsp when
  sched_sleep enters) should be preserved as long as rsp doesn't change between
  push and pop. And it doesn't — syscall_dispatch uses stack for its own frame
  but unwinds fully before returning to asm.

  BUT — under SMP, a preemption IPI during syscall_dispatch causes sched_preempt
   → do_switch. do_switch context_switches the current task away. On return, the
   task resumes. Its kstack rsp is restored. The pushed user rcx/r11/rsp are
  intact. Fine.
